   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Trinidad and Tobago</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div>

      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Country in the Caribbean
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For the islands of the sovereign state, see <a href="/wiki/Trinidad" title="Trinidad">Trinidad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tobago" title="Tobago">Tobago</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Trinidad (disambiguation)">Trinidad (disambiguation)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tobago_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Tobago (disambiguation)">Tobago (disambiguation)</a>.

      <p><b>Trinidad and Tobago</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span> ...<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Trinidad_and_Tobago.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/En-us-Trinidad_and_Tobago.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Trinidad and Tobago.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/-<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>-/</a></span></span>), officially the <b>Republic of Trinidad and Tobago</b>, is the southernmost island country in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> and is known for its fossil-fuel wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_-_Trinidad_and_Tobago_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_-_Trinidad_and_Tobago-19">[15]</a></sup> Consisting of the main islands <a href="/wiki/Trinidad" title="Trinidad">Trinidad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tobago" title="Tobago">Tobago</a>, and numerous much <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="List of islands of Trinidad and Tobago">smaller islands</a>, it is situated 130 kilometres (81 miles) south of <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">Grenada</a> and 11 kilometres (6.8 miles) off the coast of northeastern <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> It shares <a href="/wiki/Maritime_boundary" title="Maritime boundary">maritime boundaries</a> with <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a> to the northeast, Grenada to the northwest and Venezuela to the south and west.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The island of Trinidad was inhabited for centuries by native Amerindian peoples before becoming a colony in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a>, following the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> in 1498. Spanish governor <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Chac%C3%B3n" title="José María Chacón">José María Chacón</a> surrendered the island to a British fleet under the command of <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Abercromby" title="Ralph Abercromby">Sir Ralph Abercromby</a> in 1797.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael196140–42_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael196140–42-23">[19]</a></sup> During the same period, the island of Tobago changed hands among <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="British colonization of the Americas">British</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="French colonization of the Americas">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Dutch colonization of the Americas">Dutch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Courland_colonization_of_the_Americas" class="mw-redirect" title="Courland colonization of the Americas">Courlander</a> colonists more times than any other island in the Caribbean.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Trinidad and Tobago were ceded to Britain in 1802 under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Amiens" title="Treaty of Amiens">Treaty of Amiens</a> as separate states and unified in 1889.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael196152_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael196152-24">[20]</a></sup> Trinidad and Tobago obtained independence in 1962, becoming a republic in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_-_Trinidad_and_Tobago_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_-_Trinidad_and_Tobago-19">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Trinidad and Tobago has the third highest <a href="/wiki/GDP_per_capita" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP per capita">GDP per capita</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> (PPP) in the Americas after the United States and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup> It is recognised by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> as a <a href="/wiki/World_Bank_high-income_economy" title="World Bank high-income economy">high-income economy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup> Unlike most Caribbean nations and territories, which rely heavily on tourism, the economy is primarily industrial with an emphasis on petroleum and <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemicals</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[23]</a></sup> much of the nation's wealth is derived from its large reserves of oil and natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Trinidad and Tobago is well known for its African and Indian cultures, reflected in its large and famous <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Carnival" title="Trinidad and Tobago Carnival">Carnival</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hosay" title="Hosay">Hosay</a> celebrations, as well being the birthplace of <a href="/wiki/Steelpan" title="Steelpan">steelpan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Limbo_(dance)" title="Limbo (dance)">limbo</a>, and music styles such as <a href="/wiki/Calypso_music" title="Calypso music">calypso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soca_music" title="Soca music">soca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapso" title="Rapso">rapso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parang" title="Parang">parang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chutney_music" title="Chutney music">chutney</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chutney_soca" title="Chutney soca">chutney soca</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">Toponymy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Toponymy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lanzer_Joseph" title="Edward Lanzer Joseph">E. L. Joseph</a> claimed that Trinidad's Amerindian name was <i>Cairi</i> or "Land of the <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">Humming Bird</a>", derived from the <a href="/wiki/Arawak_language" title="Arawak language">Arawak</a> name for hummingbird, <i>ierèttê</i> or <i>yerettê</i>. However, other authors dispute this etymology with some claiming that <i>cairi</i> does not mean hummingbird (<i>tukusi</i> or <i>tucuchi</i> being suggested as the correct word) and some claiming that <i>kairi</i>, or <i>iere</i>, simply means <i>island</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Boomert_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boomert-38">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> renamed it "La Isla de la Trinidad" ("The Island of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>"), fulfilling a vow made before setting out on his third voyage of exploration.<sup id="cite_ref-Hart,_Marie_1965_p._13_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hart,_Marie_1965_p._13-39">[35]</a></sup> Tobago's cigar-like shape, or the use of tobacco by the native people, may have given it its Spanish name (<i>cabaco</i>, <i>tavaco</i>, <i>tobacco</i>) and possibly some of its other Amerindian names, such as <i>Aloubaéra</i> (black conch) and <i>Urupaina</i> (big snail),<sup id="cite_ref-Boomert_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boomert-38">[34]</a></sup> although the English pronunciation is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/təˈbeɪɡoʊ/</span>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="History of Trinidad and Tobago">History of Trinidad and Tobago</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_peoples">Indigenous peoples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Indigenous peoples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Both Trinidad and Tobago were originally settled by <a href="/wiki/Amerindian" class="mw-redirect" title="Amerindian">Amerindians</a> who came through South America.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> Trinidad was first settled by pre-agricultural Archaic people at least 7,000 years ago, making it the earliest settled part of the Caribbean.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Banwari_Trace" title="Banwari Trace">Banwari Trace</a> in south-west Trinidad is the oldest attested archaeological site in the Caribbean, dating to about 5000 BC. Several waves of migration occurred over the following centuries, which can be identified by differences in their archaeological remains.<sup id="cite_ref-Reid_2008_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reid_2008-41">[37]</a></sup> At the time of European contact, Trinidad was occupied by various <a href="/wiki/Arawakan" class="mw-redirect" title="Arawakan">Arawakan</a>-speaking groups including the Nepoya and Suppoya, and <a href="/wiki/Cariban_languages" title="Cariban languages">Cariban</a>-speaking groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Yao_language_(Trinidad)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yao language (Trinidad)">Yao</a>, while Tobago was occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Island_Caribs" class="mw-redirect" title="Island Caribs">Island Caribs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galibi" class="mw-redirect" title="Galibi">Galibi</a>. Trinidad was known to the native peoples as "Ieri" ("Land of the Humming Bird").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_colonisation">European colonisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: European colonisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Viceroyalty" title="Columbian Viceroyalty">Columbian Viceroyalty</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Captaincy_General_of_Cuba" title="Captaincy General of Cuba">Captaincy General of Cuba</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Christopher Columbus was the first European to see Trinidad, on his third voyage to the Americas in 1498.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams19647-8_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams19647-8-42">[38]</a></sup> He also reported seeing Tobago on the distant horizon, naming it <i>Bellaforma</i>, but did not land on the island.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmichael196114_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmichael196114-43">[39]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Raleigh_at_Trinidad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Raleigh_at_Trinidad.jpg/220px-Raleigh_at_Trinidad.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Raleigh_at_Trinidad.jpg/330px-Raleigh_at_Trinidad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Raleigh_at_Trinidad.jpg/440px-Raleigh_at_Trinidad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="412"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Raleigh_at_Trinidad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Walter_Raleigh" title="Walter Raleigh">Sir Walter Raleigh</a> raiding Spanish settlement in Trinidad in 1595
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the 1530s Antonio de Sedeño, a Spanish soldier intent on conquering the island of Trinidad, landed on its southwest coast with a small army of men, intending to subdue the Amerindian peoples of the island. Sedeño and his men fought the native peoples on many occasions, and subsequently built a fort. The next few decades were generally spent in warfare with the native peoples, until in 1592, the "Cacique" (native chief) Wannawanare (also known as Guanaguanare) granted the area around modern <a href="/wiki/Saint_Joseph,_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Saint Joseph, Trinidad and Tobago">Saint Joseph</a> to Domingo de Vera e Ibargüen, and withdrew to another part of the island.<sup id="cite_ref-Boomert_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boomert-38">[34]</a></sup> The settlement of San José de Oruña was later established by <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Berr%C3%ADo" class="mw-redirect" title="Antonio de Berrío">Antonio de Berrío</a> on this land in 1592.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup> Shortly thereafter the English sailor <a href="/wiki/Sir_Walter_Raleigh" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Walter Raleigh">Sir Walter Raleigh</a> arrived in Trinidad on 22 March 1595 <a href="/wiki/Raleigh%27s_El_Dorado_Expedition" title="Raleigh's El Dorado Expedition">in search</a> of the long-rumoured "<a href="/wiki/El_Dorado" title="El Dorado">El Dorado</a>" ("City of Gold") supposedly located in South America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup> He attacked San José, captured and interrogated Antonio de Berrío, and obtained much information from him and from the Cacique Topiawari; Raleigh then went on his way, and Spanish authority was restored.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams196411,18_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams196411,18-44">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Meanwhile, there were numerous attempts by European powers to settle Tobago during the 1620-40s, with the Dutch, English and Couronians (people from the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Courland_and_Semigallia" title="Duchy of Courland and Semigallia">Duchy of Courland and Semigallia</a>, now part of <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>) all attempting to colonise the island with little success.<sup id="cite_ref-dutch_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dutch-45">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018196_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018196-46">[42]</a></sup> From 1654 the Dutch and Courlanders managed to gain a more secure foothold, later joined by several hundred French settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-dutch_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dutch-45">[41]</a></sup> A plantation economy developed based on the production of sugar, indigo and rum, worked by large numbers of African slaves who soon came to vastly outnumber the European colonists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018196_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018196-46">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dutch_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dutch-45">[41]</a></sup> Large numbers of forts were constructed as Tobago became a source of contention between France, Netherlands and Britain, with the island changing hands some 31 times prior to 1814, a situation exacerbated by widespread piracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018196_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018196-46">[42]</a></sup> The British managed to hold Tobago from 1762 to 1781, whereupon it was captured by the French, who ruled until 1793 when Britain re-captured the island.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018196_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018196-46">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The 17th century on Trinidad passed largely without major incident, but sustained attempts by the Spaniards to control and rule over the Amerindians were often fiercely resisted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup> In 1687 the <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Catalan_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalan people">Catalan</a> <a href="/wiki/Capuchin_friars" class="mw-redirect" title="Capuchin friars">Capuchin friars</a> were given responsibility for <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">the conversions</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous people</a> of <a href="/wiki/Trinidad" title="Trinidad">Trinidad</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Guianas" class="mw-redirect" title="Guianas">Guianas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup> They founded several missions in Trinidad, supported and richly funded by the state, which also granted <i><a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">encomienda</a></i> right to them over the native peoples, in which the native peoples were forced to provide labour for the Spanish.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup> One such mission was <i>Santa Rosa de Arima,</i> established in 1689, when Amerindians from the former <i>encomiendas</i> of <a href="/wiki/Tacarigua" title="Tacarigua">Tacarigua</a> and <i>Arauca</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arouca,_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Arouca, Trinidad and Tobago">Arouca</a>) were relocated further west.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Escalating tensions between the Spaniards and Amerindians culminated in violence 1689, when Amerindians in the San Rafael encomienda rebelled and killed several priests, attacked a church, and killed the Spanish governor <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_Le%C3%B3n_y_Echales" title="José de León y Echales">José de León y Echales</a>. Among those killed in the governor's party was Juan Mazien de Sotomayor, missionary priest to the Nepuyo villages of Cuara, Tacarigua and Arauca.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup> The Spanish retaliated severely, slaughtering hundreds of native peoples in an event that became known as the <a href="/wiki/Arena_massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Arena massacre">Arena massacre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup> As a result, continuing Spanish slave-raiding, and the devastating impact of introduced disease to which they had no immunity, the native population was virtually wiped out by the end of the following century.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249_40-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018249-40">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During this period Trinidad was an island province belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Viceroyalty of New Spain">Viceroyalty of New Spain</a>, together with Central America, present-day <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> and what would later become the southwestern <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Besson,_2000_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Besson,_2000-49">[45]</a></sup> In 1757 the capital was moved from San José de Oruña to Puerto de España (modern <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Spain" title="Port of Spain">Port of Spain</a>) following several pirate attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides201855_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides201855-50">[46]</a></sup> However the Spanish never made any concerted effort to colonise the islands; Trinidad in this period was still mostly forest, populated by a few Spaniards with a handful of slaves and a few thousand Amerindians.<sup id="cite_ref-Besson,_2000_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Besson,_2000-49">[45]</a></sup> Indeed, the population in 1777 was only 1,400, and Spanish colonisation in Trinidad remained tenuous.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Influx_of_French_settlers">Influx of French settlers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Influx of French settlers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/French_West_Indies" title="French West Indies">French West Indies</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1777, the captain general <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Unzaga" title="Luis de Unzaga">Luis de Unzaga</a> 'le Conciliateur', married to a French Creole, allowed free trade in Trinidad, attracting French settlers and its economy improved notably.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[47]</a></sup> Since Trinidad was considered underpopulated, Roume de St. Laurent, a Frenchman living in <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">Grenada</a>, was able to obtain a <a href="/wiki/Cedula_of_Population" title="Cedula of Population"><i>Cédula de Población</i></a> from the Spanish king <a href="/wiki/Charles_III_of_Spain" title="Charles III of Spain">Charles III</a> on 4 November 1783.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018250_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018250-52">[48]</a></sup> A <i>Cédula de Población</i> had previously been granted in 1776 by the king, but had not shown results, and therefore the new Cédula was more generous.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> It granted free land and tax exemption for 10 years to Roman Catholic foreign settlers who were willing to swear allegiance to the King of Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> The Spanish also gave many incentives to lure settlers to the island, including exemption from taxes for ten years and land grants in accordance with the terms set out in the <i>Cédula</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[49]</a></sup> The land grant was 30 fanegas (13 hectares/32 acres) for each free man, woman and child and half of that for each slave that they brought with them. The Spanish sent a new governor, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Chac%C3%B3n" title="José María Chacón">José María Chacón</a>, to implement the terms of the new <i>cédula</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018250_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018250-52">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>It was fortuitous that the Cédula was issued only a few years before the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. During that period of upheaval, French planters with their slaves, free <a href="/wiki/Coloureds" title="Coloureds">coloureds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mulattos" class="mw-redirect" title="Mulattos">mulattos</a> from the neighbouring islands of <a href="/wiki/Martinique" title="Martinique">Martinique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lucia" title="Saint Lucia">Saint Lucia</a>, Grenada, <a href="/wiki/Guadeloupe" title="Guadeloupe">Guadeloupe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dominica" title="Dominica">Dominica</a> migrated to Trinidad, where they established an agriculture-based economy (sugar and cocoa).<sup id="cite_ref-Besson,_2000_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Besson,_2000-49">[45]</a></sup> These new immigrants established local communities in <a href="/wiki/Blanchisseuse" title="Blanchisseuse">Blanchisseuse</a>, Champs Fleurs, <a href="/wiki/Paramin" title="Paramin">Paramin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup> Cascade, <a href="/wiki/Carenage" title="Carenage">Carenage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laventille" title="Laventille">Laventille</a>. </p>
      <p>As a result, Trinidad's population jumped to over 15,000 by the end of 1789, and by 1797 the population of <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Spain" title="Port of Spain">Port of Spain</a> had increased from under 3,000 to 10,422 in just five years, with a varied population of mixed race individuals, Spaniards, Africans, French republican soldiers, retired pirates and French nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-Besson,_2000_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Besson,_2000-49">[45]</a></sup> The total population of Trinidad was 17,718, of which 2,151 were of European ancestry, 4,476 were "free blacks and people of colour", 10,009 were enslaved people and 1,082 <a href="/wiki/Amerindians" class="mw-redirect" title="Amerindians">Amerindians</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The sparse settlement and slow rate of population-increase during Spanish rule (and even later during British rule) made Trinidad one of the less populated colonies of the West Indies, with the least developed plantation infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-Brereton_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brereton-55">[51]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_rule">British rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: British rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">British West Indies</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Windward_Islands" title="British Windward Islands">British Windward Islands</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Trinidad_Ralph_Abercromby.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trinidad_Ralph_Abercromby.JPG/220px-Trinidad_Ralph_Abercromby.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trinidad_Ralph_Abercromby.JPG/330px-Trinidad_Ralph_Abercromby.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trinidad_Ralph_Abercromby.JPG/440px-Trinidad_Ralph_Abercromby.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Trinidad_Ralph_Abercromby.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A medallion showing the capture of Trinidad and Tobago by the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> in 1797.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The British had begun to take a keen interest in Trinidad, and in 1797 a British force led by General <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Abercromby" title="Ralph Abercromby">Sir Ralph Abercromby</a> launched an <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Trinidad_(1797)" title="Invasion of Trinidad (1797)">invasion of Trinidad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018251_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018251-56">[52]</a></sup> His squadron sailed through the Bocas and anchored off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Chaguaramas,_Trinidad" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaguaramas, Trinidad">Chaguaramas</a>. Seriously outnumbered, Chacón decided to capitulate to British without fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018251_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018251-56">[52]</a></sup> Trinidad thus became a British <a href="/wiki/Crown_colony" title="Crown colony">crown colony</a>, with a largely French-speaking population and Spanish laws.<sup id="cite_ref-Besson,_2000_49-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Besson,_2000-49">[45]</a></sup> British rule was later formalised under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Amiens" title="Treaty of Amiens">Treaty of Amiens</a> (1802).<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018251_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018251-56">[52]</a></sup> The colony's first British governor was <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Picton" title="Thomas Picton">Thomas Picton</a>, however his heavy-handed approach to enforcing British authority, including the use of torture and <a href="/wiki/Arbitrary_arrest_and_detention" title="Arbitrary arrest and detention">arbitrary arrest</a>, led to his being recalled.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018251_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018251-56">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>British rule led to an influx of settlers from the United Kingdom and the British colonies of the Eastern Caribbean. English, Scots, Irish, German and Italian families arrived, as well as some free blacks known as "<a href="/wiki/Merikins" title="Merikins">Merikins</a>" who had fought for Britain in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> and were granted land in southern Trinidad.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252-59">[55]</a></sup> Under British rule, new states were created and the importation of slaves increased, however by this time support for <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">abolitionism</a> had vastly increased and in England the slave trade was under attack.<sup id="cite_ref-Brereton_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brereton-55">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams1964_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams1964-60">[56]</a></sup> Slavery was <a href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833" title="Slavery Abolition Act 1833">abolished in 1833</a>, after which former slaves served an "<a href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833#The_Act" title="Slavery Abolition Act 1833">apprenticeship</a>" period. In 1837 Daaga, a West African slave trader who had been captured by Portuguese slavers and later rescued by the British navy, was conscripted into the local regiment. Daaga and a group of his compatriots mutinied at the barracks in St Joseph and set out eastward in an attempt to return to their homeland. The mutineers were ambushed by a militia unit just outside the town of Arima. The revolt was crushed at the cost of some 40 dead, and Daaga and his party were later executed at St Joseph.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018126_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018126-61">[57]</a></sup> The apprenticeship system ended on 1 August 1838 with full emancipation.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252-59">[55]</a></sup> An overview of the populations statistics in 1838, however, clearly reveals the contrast between Trinidad and its neighbouring islands: upon emancipation of the slaves in 1838, Trinidad had only 17,439 slaves, with 80% of slave owners having enslaved fewer than 10 people each.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams196484–85_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams196484–85-62">[58]</a></sup> In contrast, at twice the size of Trinidad, Jamaica had roughly 360,000 slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrival_of_Indian_Indentured_Labourers">Arrival of Indian Indentured Labourers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Arrival of Indian Indentured Labourers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Newly_arrived_coolies_in_Trinidad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Newly_arrived_coolies_in_Trinidad.jpg/220px-Newly_arrived_coolies_in_Trinidad.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Newly_arrived_coolies_in_Trinidad.jpg/330px-Newly_arrived_coolies_in_Trinidad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Newly_arrived_coolies_in_Trinidad.jpg/440px-Newly_arrived_coolies_in_Trinidad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2077" data-file-height="1380"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Newly_arrived_coolies_in_Trinidad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Newly arrived <a href="/wiki/Indian_indenture_system" title="Indian indenture system">indentured Indian labourers</a> in Trinidad and Tobago.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After the African slaves were emancipated many refused to continue working on the plantations, often moving out to urban areas such as Laventille and <a href="/wiki/Belmont,_Port_of_Spain" title="Belmont, Port of Spain">Belmont</a> to the east of Port of Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252-59">[55]</a></sup> As a result, a severe agricultural labour shortage emerged. The British filled this gap by instituting a system of <a href="/wiki/Indentureship" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentureship">indentureship</a>. Various nationalities were contracted under this system, including Indians, Chinese, and Portuguese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018253_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018253-64">[60]</a></sup> Of these, the East Indians were imported in the largest numbers, starting from 1 May 1845, when 225 Indians were brought in the first shipment to Trinidad on the <i><a href="/wiki/Fatel_Razack" title="Fatel Razack">Fatel Razack</a></i>, a Muslim-owned vessel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252-59">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> Indentureship of the Indians lasted from 1845 to 1917, during which time more than 147,000 Indians came to Trinidad to work on sugarcane plantations.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Indentureship contracts were sometimes exploitative, to such an extent that historians such as Hugh Tinker were to call it "a new system of slavery". Despite these descriptions, it was not truly a new form of slavery, as workers were paid, contracts were finite, and the idea of an individual being another's property had been eliminated when slavery was abolished.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup> In addition, employers of indentured labour had no legal right to flog or whip their workers; the main legal sanction for the enforcement of the indenture laws was prosecution in the courts, followed by fines or (more likely) jail sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup> People were contracted for a period of five years, with a daily wage as low as 25 cents in the early 20th century, and they were guaranteed return passage to India at the end of their contract period. However, <a href="/wiki/Coercion" title="Coercion">coercive means</a> were often used to retain labourers, and the <a href="/wiki/Indentured_servant" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured servant">indentureship contracts</a> were soon extended to 10 years from 1854 after the planters complained that they were losing their labour too early.<sup id="cite_ref-Brereton_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brereton-55">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018252-59">[55]</a></sup> In lieu of the return passage, the British authorities soon began offering portions of land to encourage settlement, and by 1902, more than half of the sugar cane in Trinidad was being produced by independent cane farmers; the majority of which were Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup> Despite the trying conditions experienced under the indenture system, about 90% of the Indian immigrants chose, at the end of their contracted periods of indenture, to make Trinidad their permanent home.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-70">[66]</a></sup> East Indians entering the colony were also subject to certain crown laws which segregated them from the rest of Trinidad's population, such as the requirement that they carry a pass with them if they left the plantations, and that if freed, they carry their "Free Papers" or certificate indicating completion of the indenture period.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago_(1889%E2%80%931958).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago_%281889%E2%80%931958%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago_%281889%E2%80%931958%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago_%281889%E2%80%931958%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago_%281889%E2%80%931958%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago_%281889%E2%80%931958%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago_%281889%E2%80%931958%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago_(1889%E2%80%931958).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Colonial flag of Trinidad and Tobago, 1889–1958
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Few Indians settled on Tobago however, and the descendants of African slaves continued to form the majority of the island's population. An ongoing economic slump in the middle-to-late 19th century caused widespread poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018239_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018239-72">[68]</a></sup> Discontent erupted into rioting on the <a href="/wiki/Roxborough,_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Roxborough, Trinidad and Tobago">Roxborough</a> plantation in 1876, in an event known as the Belmanna Uprising after a policeman who was killed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018239_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018239-72">[68]</a></sup> The British eventually managed to restore control, however as a result of the disturbances Tobago's Legislative Assembly voted to dissolve itself and the island became a Crown colony in 1877.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018239_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018239-72">[68]</a></sup> With the sugar industry in a state of near-collapse and the island no longer profitable, the British attached Tobago to their Trinidad colony in 1889.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255-73">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams1964149_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams1964149-74">[70]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century">Early 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Early 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In 1903, <a href="/wiki/1903_water_riots" title="1903 water riots">a protest</a> against the introduction of new water rates in Port of Spain erupted into rioting; 18 people were shot dead, and the Red House (the government headquarters) was damaged by fire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255-73">[69]</a></sup> A local elected assembly with some limited powers was introduced in 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255-73">[69]</a></sup> Economically Trinidad and Tobago remained a predominantly agricultural colony; alongside sugarcane, the cacao (<a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa</a>) crop also contributed greatly to economic earnings in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. </p>
      <p>In November 1919, the dockworkers went on strike over bad management practices, low wages compared to a higher cost of living.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKiely199666_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKiely199666-75">[71]</a></sup> Strikebreakers were brought in to keep a minimum of goods moving through the ports. On 1 December 1919, the striking dockworkers rushed the harbour and chased off the strikebreakers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKiely199666_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKiely199666-75">[71]</a></sup> They then proceeded to march on the government buildings in Port of Spain. Other unions and workers, many with the same grievances, joined the dock worker's strike making it a General Strike.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKiely199666_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKiely199666-75">[71]</a></sup> Violence broke out and was only put down with help from the sailors of British Naval ship <a href="/wiki/HMS_Calcutta_(D82)" title="HMS Calcutta (D82)">HMS&nbsp;<i>Calcutta</i></a>. The unity brought upon by the strike was the first time of cooperation between the various ethnic groups of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKiely199667_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKiely199667-76">[72]</a></sup> Historian Brinsley Samaroo says that the 1919 strikes "seem to indicate that there was a growing class consciousness after the war and this transcended racial feelings at times."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKiely199667_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKiely199667-76">[72]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>However, in the 1920s, the collapse of the sugarcane industry, concomitant with the failure of the cocoa industry, resulted in widespread depression among the rural and agricultural workers in Trinidad, and encouraged the rise of a labour movement. Conditions on the islands worsened in the 1930s with the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, with an outbreak of <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indian_labour_unrest_of_1934%E2%80%9339" class="mw-redirect" title="British West Indian labour unrest of 1934–39">labour riots</a> occurring in 1937 which resulted in several deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018185_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018185-77">[73]</a></sup> The labour movement aimed to unite the urban working class and agricultural labour class; the key figures being <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Andrew_Cipriani" title="Arthur Andrew Cipriani">Arthur Cipriani</a>, who led the Trinidad Workingmen's Association (TWA), and <a href="/wiki/Tubal_Uriah_Butler" title="Tubal Uriah Butler">Tubal Uriah "Buzz" Butler</a> of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire_Citizens%27_and_Workers%27_Home_Rule_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="British Empire Citizens' and Workers' Home Rule Party">British Empire Citizens' and Workers' Home Rule Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018185_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018185-77">[73]</a></sup> As the movement developed calls for greater autonomy from British colonial rule became widespread; this effort was severely undermined by the British Home Office and by the British-educated Trinidadian elite, many of whom were descended from the plantocracy class. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Waller_AFB_mess_hall_sign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Waller_AFB_mess_hall_sign.jpg/220px-Waller_AFB_mess_hall_sign.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Waller_AFB_mess_hall_sign.jpg/330px-Waller_AFB_mess_hall_sign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Waller_AFB_mess_hall_sign.jpg/440px-Waller_AFB_mess_hall_sign.jpg 2x" data-file-width="698" data-file-height="507"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Waller_AFB_mess_hall_sign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A soldier at <a href="/wiki/Waller_Air_Force_Base" title="Waller Air Force Base">Waller Air Force Base</a>, leased by Britain to America in the 1940s
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Petroleum had been discovered in 1857, but became economically significant only in the 1930s and afterwards as a result of the collapse of sugarcane and cocoa, and increasing industrialisation.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WDL_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-80">[76]</a></sup> By the 1950s petroleum had become a staple in Trinidad's export market, and was responsible for a growing middle class among all sections of the Trinidad population. The collapse of Trinidad's major agricultural commodities, followed by the Depression, and the rise of the oil economy, led to major changes in the country's social structure. </p>
      <p>The presence of American <a href="/wiki/Destroyers_for_Bases_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Destroyers for Bases Agreement">military bases</a> in Chaguaramas and <a href="/wiki/Cumuto" title="Cumuto">Cumuto</a> in Trinidad during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> had a profound effect on society. The Americans vastly improved the infrastructure on Trinidad and provided many locals with well-paying jobs; however the social effects of having so many young soldiers stationed on the island, as well as their often unconcealed racial prejudice, caused resentment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255_73-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255-73">[69]</a></sup> The Americans left in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides201896_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides201896-81">[77]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the post-war period the British began a process of decolonisation across the British Empire. In 1945 universal suffrage was introduced to Trinidad and Tobago.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255_73-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018255-73">[69]</a></sup> Political parties emerged on the island, however these were largely divided along racial lines: Afro-Trinidadians and Tobagonians primarily supported the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_National_Movement" title="People's National Movement">People's National Movement</a> (PNM), formed in 1956 by <a href="/wiki/Eric_Williams" title="Eric Williams">Eric Williams</a>, with Indo-Trinidadians and Tobagonians mostly supporting the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_(Trinidad_and_Tobago)" title="People's Democratic Party (Trinidad and Tobago)">People's Democratic Party</a> (PDP), formed in 1953 by <a href="/wiki/Bhadase_Maraj" title="Bhadase Maraj">Bhadase Sagan Maraj</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[78]</a></sup> which later merged into the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Labour_Party_(Trinidad_and_Tobago)" title="Democratic Labour Party (Trinidad and Tobago)">Democratic Labour Party</a> (DLP) in 1957.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256-83">[79]</a></sup> Britain's Caribbean colonies formed the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies_Federation" title="West Indies Federation">West Indies Federation</a> in 1958 as a vehicle for independence, however the Federation dissolved after Jamaica withdrew following a <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_Federation_of_the_West_Indies_membership_referendum,_1961" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamaican Federation of the West Indies membership referendum, 1961">membership referendum</a> in 1961. The government of Trinidad and Tobago subsequently chose to seek independence from the United Kingdom on its own.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_era">Contemporary era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Contemporary era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/West_Indies_Federation" title="West Indies Federation">West Indies Federation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Independence_Act_1962" title="Trinidad and Tobago Independence Act 1962">Trinidad and Tobago Independence Act 1962</a>
      </div> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:197px;max-width:197px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="theader">
          Leaders at Independence
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:95px;max-width:95px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Elizabeth_II_1959.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Queen_Elizabeth_II_1959.jpg/93px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_1959.jpg" decoding="async" width="93" height="119" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Queen_Elizabeth_II_1959.jpg/140px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_1959.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Queen_Elizabeth_II_1959.jpg/186px-Queen_Elizabeth_II_1959.jpg 2x" data-file-width="730" data-file-height="932"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">
           <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>
           <br><small><a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Queen of Trinidad and Tobago">Queen</a></small>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:98px;max-width:98px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Eric_Williams_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Eric_Williams_%28cropped%29.jpg/96px-Eric_Williams_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="96" height="119" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Eric_Williams_%28cropped%29.jpg/144px-Eric_Williams_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Eric_Williams_%28cropped%29.jpg/192px-Eric_Williams_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="489" data-file-height="606"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">
           <a href="/wiki/Eric_Williams" title="Eric Williams">Eric Williams</a>
           <br><small><a href="/wiki/List_of_prime_ministers_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="List of prime ministers of Trinidad and Tobago">Prime Minister</a></small>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Trinidad and Tobago gained its <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Independence_Act_1962" title="Trinidad and Tobago Independence Act 1962">independence</a> from the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> on 31 August 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WDL_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-80">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> remained head of state as <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Queen of Trinidad and Tobago">Queen of Trinidad and Tobago</a>, represented locally by <a href="/wiki/List_of_heads_of_state_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago#Governor-General" title="List of heads of state of Trinidad and Tobago">Governor-General</a> <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Hochoy" title="Solomon Hochoy">Solomon Hochoy</a>. Eric Williams of the PNM, a noted historian and intellectual widely regarded as <i><a href="/wiki/Father_of_the_Nation" title="Father of the Nation">The Father of The Nation</a></i>, became the first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Prime Ministers of Trinidad and Tobago">Prime Minister</a>, serving in that capacity uninterrupted until 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> The dominant figure in the opposition in the early independence years was <a href="/wiki/Rudranath_Capildeo" title="Rudranath Capildeo">Rudranath Capildeo</a> of the DLP. The 1960s saw the rise of a <a href="/wiki/Black_Power_Revolution" title="Black Power Revolution">Black Power movement</a>, inspired in part by the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> in the United States. Protests and strikes became common, with events coming to head in April 1970 when police shot dead a protester named Basil Davis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256-83">[79]</a></sup> Fearing a breakdown of law and order, Prime Minister Williams declared a state of emergency and arrested many of the Black Power leaders. Some army leaders who were sympathetic to the Black Power movement, notably <a href="/wiki/Raffique_Shah" title="Raffique Shah">Raffique Shah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rex_Lassalle" title="Rex Lassalle">Rex Lassalle</a>, attempted to mutiny; however, this was quashed by the <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinidad and Tobago Coast Guard">Trinidad and Tobago Coast Guard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256-83">[79]</a></sup> Williams and the PNM retained power, largely due to divisions in the opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256_83-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256-83">[79]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1963 Tobago was struck by <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Flora" title="Hurricane Flora">Hurricane Flora</a>, which killed 30 people and resulted in enormous destruction across the island.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018236_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018236-85">[81]</a></sup> Partly as a result of this, tourism came to replace agriculture as the island's main income earner in the subsequent decades.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018236_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018236-85">[81]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Between the years 1972 and 1983, the country profited greatly from the rising price of oil and the discovery of vast new oil deposits in its territorial waters, resulting in an economic boom that increased living standards greatly.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256_83-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256-83">[79]</a></sup> In 1976 the country became a republic within the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>, though it retained the <a href="/wiki/Judicial_Committee_of_the_Privy_Council" title="Judicial Committee of the Privy Council">Judicial Committee of the Privy Council</a> as its final appellate court.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> The position of governor-general was replaced with that of <a href="/wiki/President_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="President of Trinidad and Tobago">President</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ellis_Clarke" title="Ellis Clarke">Ellis Clarke</a> was the first to hold this largely ceremonial role.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup> Tobago was granted limited self-rule with the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Tobago_House_of_Assembly" title="Tobago House of Assembly">Tobago House of Assembly</a> in 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018239_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018239-72">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Williams died in 1981, being replaced by <a href="/wiki/George_Chambers" title="George Chambers">George Chambers</a> who led the country until 1986. By this time a fall in the price of oil had resulted in a recession, causing rising inflation and unemployment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018257_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018257-87">[83]</a></sup> The main opposition parties united under the banner of <a href="/wiki/National_Alliance_for_Reconstruction" title="National Alliance for Reconstruction">National Alliance for Reconstruction</a> (NAR) and won the <a href="/wiki/1986_Trinidad_and_Tobago_general_election" title="1986 Trinidad and Tobago general election">1986 Trinidad and Tobago general election</a>, with NAR leader <a href="/wiki/A._N._R._Robinson" title="A. N. R. Robinson">A. N. R. Robinson</a> becoming the new Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-N1_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-N1-88">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256_83-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018256-83">[79]</a></sup> Robinson was unable to hold together the fragile NAR coalition, and social unrest was caused by his economic reforms, such as devaluing the currency and implementing an <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> <a href="/wiki/Structural_Adjustment_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="Structural Adjustment Program">Structural Adjustment Program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> In 1990 114 members of the <a href="/wiki/Jamaat_al_Muslimeen" title="Jamaat al Muslimeen">Jamaat al Muslimeen</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Yasin_Abu_Bakr" title="Yasin Abu Bakr">Yasin Abu Bakr</a> (formerly known as Lennox Phillip) stormed the <a href="/wiki/The_Red_House_(Trinidad_and_Tobago)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Red House (Trinidad and Tobago)">Red House</a> (the seat of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Parliament of Trinidad and Tobago">Parliament</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Television" title="Trinidad and Tobago Television">Trinidad and Tobago Television</a>, the only television station in the country at the time, <a href="/wiki/Jamaat_al_Muslimeen_coup_attempt" title="Jamaat al Muslimeen coup attempt">holding Robinson and country's government hostage</a> for six days before surrendering.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup> The coup leaders were promised amnesty, but upon their surrender they were then arrested, but later released after protracted legal wrangling.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018253_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018253-64">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The PNM under <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Manning" title="Patrick Manning">Patrick Manning</a> returned to power following the <a href="/wiki/1991_Trinidad_and_Tobago_general_election" title="1991 Trinidad and Tobago general election">1991 Trinidad and Tobago general election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> Hoping to capitalise on an improvement in the economy, Manning called <a href="/wiki/1995_Trinidad_and_Tobago_general_election" title="1995 Trinidad and Tobago general election">an early election</a> in 1995, however, this resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>. Two NAR representatives backed the opposition <a href="/wiki/United_National_Congress" title="United National Congress">United National Congress</a> (UNC), which had split off from the NAR in 1989, and they thus took power under <a href="/wiki/Basdeo_Panday" title="Basdeo Panday">Basdeo Panday</a>, who became the country's first Indo-Trinidadian Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018257_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018257-87">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup> After a period of political confusion caused by a series of inconclusive election results, Patrick Manning returned to power in 2001, retaining that position until 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since 2003 the country entered a second oil boom, and petroleum, petrochemicals and natural gas continue to be the backbone of the economy. Tourism and the public service are the mainstay of the economy of Tobago, though authorities have attempted to diversify the island's economy.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup> A corruption scandal resulted in Manning's defeat by the newly formed <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Partnership" title="People's Partnership">People's Partnership</a> coalition in 2010, with <a href="/wiki/Kamla_Persad-Bissessar" title="Kamla Persad-Bissessar">Kamla Persad-Bissessar</a> becoming the country's first female Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018258_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018258-92">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup> However, corruption allegations bedevilled the new administration, and the PP were defeated in 2015 by the PNM under <a href="/wiki/Keith_Rowley" title="Keith Rowley">Keith Rowley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018259_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERough_Guides2018259-95">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup> In August 2020, the governing People's National Movement won general <a href="/wiki/2020_Trinidad_and_Tobago_general_election" title="2020 Trinidad and Tobago general election">election</a>, meaning the incumbent Prime Minister Keith Rowley will serve a second term.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Geography of Trinidad and Tobago">Geography of Trinidad and Tobago</a>
      </div> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r992277484">.mw-parser-output .stack-container{box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-left{float:left;clear:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-right{float:right;clear:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-clear-left{float:left;clear:left;margin-right:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-clear-right{float:right;clear:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-left{float:left;margin-right:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-right{float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-container>div{margin:1px;overflow:hidden}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .stack-container{float:none;width:100%}}</style>
      <div class="mw-stack stack-container stack-right">
       <div> 
        <div class="thumb tright">
         <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Td-map.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Td-map.gif/280px-Td-map.gif" decoding="async" width="280" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/01/Td-map.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="342" data-file-height="367"></a> 
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <div class="magnify">
            <a href="/wiki/File:Td-map.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
           </div>A map of Trinidad and Tobago
          </div>
         </div>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumb tright">
         <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Trinidad_Topography.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Trinidad_Topography.png/280px-Trinidad_Topography.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Trinidad_Topography.png/420px-Trinidad_Topography.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Trinidad_Topography.png/560px-Trinidad_Topography.png 2x" data-file-width="1750" data-file-height="1704"></a> 
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <div class="magnify">
            <a href="/wiki/File:Trinidad_Topography.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
           </div>Trinidad and Tobago's topography
          </div>
         </div>
        </div> 
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Trinidad and Tobago is situated between 10° 2' and 11° 12' N <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> and 60° 30' and 61° 56' W <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a> to the north, the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> to the east and south, and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Paria" title="Gulf of Paria">Gulf of Paria</a> to the west. It is located in the far south-east of the Caribbean region, with the island of Trinidad being just 11 kilometres (6.8&nbsp;mi) off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> in mainland <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Channel" title="Columbus Channel">Columbus Channel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> The islands are a physiographical extension of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-98">[94]</a></sup> Covering an area of 5,128&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (1,980&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi),<sup id="cite_ref-back_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-back-99">[95]</a></sup> the country consists of two main islands, Trinidad and <a href="/wiki/Tobago" title="Tobago">Tobago</a>, separated by a 20-mile (32&nbsp;km) strait, plus a number of much <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="List of islands of Trinidad and Tobago">smaller islands</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Chacachacare" title="Chacachacare">Chacachacare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monos" title="Monos">Monos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huevos" title="Huevos">Huevos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_Grande" title="Gaspar Grande">Gaspar Grande</a> (or Gasparee), <a href="/wiki/Little_Tobago" title="Little Tobago">Little Tobago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Giles_Island" title="Saint Giles Island">Saint Giles Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Trinidad is 4,768&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (1,841&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) in area (comprising 93.0% of the country's total area) with an average length of 80 kilometres (50&nbsp;mi) and an average width of 59 kilometres (37&nbsp;mi). Tobago has an area of about 300&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (120&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), or 5.8% of the country's area, is 41&nbsp;km (25&nbsp;mi) long and 12&nbsp;km (7.5&nbsp;mi) at its greatest width. Trinidad and Tobago lie on the continental shelf of South America, and are thus geologically considered to lie entirely in South America.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The terrain of the islands is a mixture of mountains and plains.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_-_Trinidad_and_Tobago_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_-_Trinidad_and_Tobago-19">[15]</a></sup> On Trinidad the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Range" title="Northern Range">Northern Range</a> runs parallel with the north coast, and contains the country's highest peak (<a href="/wiki/El_Cerro_del_Aripo" title="El Cerro del Aripo">El Cerro del Aripo</a>), which is 940 metres (3,080&nbsp;ft) above sea level,<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Factbook_-_Trinidad_and_Tobago_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Factbook_-_Trinidad_and_Tobago-19">[15]</a></sup> and second highest (<a href="/wiki/El_Tucuche" title="El Tucuche">El Tucuche</a>, 936 metres (3,071&nbsp;ft)).<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> The rest of the island is generally flatter, excluding the <a href="/wiki/Central_Range,_Trinidad_and_Tobago" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Range, Trinidad and Tobago">Central Range</a> and Montserrat Hills in the centre of the island and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Range" title="Southern Range">Southern Range</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Hills" title="Trinity Hills">Trinity Hills</a> in the south. The three mountain ranges determine the drainage pattern of Trinidad.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-98">[94]</a></sup> The east coast is noted for its beaches, most notably <a href="/wiki/Manzanilla_Beach,_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Manzanilla Beach, Trinidad and Tobago">Manzanilla Beach</a>. The island contains several large swamp areas, such as the <a href="/wiki/Caroni_Swamp" title="Caroni Swamp">Caroni Swamp</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nariva_Swamp" title="Nariva Swamp">Nariva Swamp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EBTT_20-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBTT-20">[16]</a></sup> Major bodies of water on Trinidad include the <a href="/wiki/Hollis_Reservoir" title="Hollis Reservoir">Hollis Reservoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Navet_Dam" title="Navet Dam">Navet Reservoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caroni%E2%80%93Arena_Dam" title="Caroni–Arena Dam">Caroni Reservoir</a>. Trinidad is made up of a variety of soil types, the majority being fine sands and heavy clays. The alluvial valleys of the Northern Range and the soils of the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Corridor" title="East–West Corridor">East–West Corridor</a> are the most fertile.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources.
